--- SUMMARY ---

Summary

The opening session of "AI in AEC 2026," led by Vesa Järvinen, offered a detailed overview of the event's structure, tools, and themes, emphasizing collaboration and innovation in the architecture, engineering, and construction (AEC) industry. The hybrid format, combining virtual and on-site participation, has evolved since its inception during the COVID-19 pandemic, attracting over 300 participants from 38 countries, the highest attendance in the event's history. Attendees were encouraged to use the event app, which facilitates polls, provides access to shared materials, and features a "snapsight" tool for automatic note-taking and key takeaways.

Partner contributions were acknowledged for enabling moderate pricing, supporting a diverse audience to engage with 50 presentations from four continents. Keynote presentations, longer than regular sessions, were highlighted as pivotal moments, beginning with Dr. Sam Solfakarian's insights into AI's transformative potential. His talk set the stage for discussions on AI implementation, multi-agent workflows, sustainable business models, and computational design. The closing keynote by Sigrid Perkan will explore future construction sites, linking physical AI advancements to industry evolution.

Logistical details, including the evening dinner located 26 minutes from the venue, were shared to ensure smooth planning. Feedback links will be provided at the event's conclusion to gather attendee input for future improvements. Universities' role in AI education was emphasized, with a call for young professionals skilled in AI to address resource shortages. Risto Siilasmaa's advice to aim for transformative AI goals rather than incremental improvements resonated throughout the session.

Innovative tools like Flowcase, which streamlines employee skill management, and SVECO GPT, focusing on green transition challenges, were showcased. HNTB's AI services for infrastructure, including digital twin applications, demonstrated practical AI applications in the industry. The CIB task group on AI in construction invited participants to collaborate on advancing AI adoption globally. Overall, the session underscored the importance of ambitious goals, collaboration, and leveraging AI to redefine the future of the AEC industry.

--- SUMMARY ---

Summary

At the "AI in AEC 2026" event, Sam Zolfagharian delivered the keynote session, "Opening Session Keynote 1 - The Future of AEC Work in an AI-Driven Environment," addressing the profound impact of algorithmic intelligence on the architecture, engineering, and construction (AEC) industry. Sam explored how companies are investing in AI tools, such as GPT licenses and AI agents, yet often fail to achieve ROI due to outdated systems designed for human limitations rather than AI capabilities.

Sam emphasized the transition from human intelligence to algorithmic intelligence, where AI analyzes vast datasets to provide actionable insights. She illustrated this with examples of AI tools revolutionizing proposal analysis, quantity takeoff, and code checking, reducing weeks of manual effort to minutes. Disruptions in other industries, such as Adobe being challenged by MidJourney and Google by Perplexity, highlight AI's ability to reshape business models. She also cited Uber's 250 AI models and the proliferation of self-driving cars in San Francisco as evidence of AI's transformative reach.

The session underscored the importance of fostering a culture of innovation, with examples like Citibank's AI champions and 3M's 15% innovation rule. Sam praised SSOE's proactive approach, combining training and experimentation to lead the industry in AI adoption. She highlighted how companies like Levi's, JCDouka, and Wetrim are leveraging AI for personalization, insights, and scaling expertise, showcasing AI's potential to create new value propositions and expand markets.

Sam urged attendees to rethink their business and operating models to fully embrace AI's transformative potential. She shared how AIG optimized risk analysis workflows and PepsiCo used digital twins to enhance factory layouts, demonstrating AI's ability to drive efficiency and innovation. He concluded by emphasizing that AI is not merely a tool for incremental improvement but a catalyst for organizational and industry-wide transformation, enabling smaller teams to achieve greater impact through intelligent collaboration. Sam left the audience with the challenge to become change-makers in this new era, leveraging AI to redefine the future of work in the AEC industry.

Speakers

SZ

Sam Zolfagharian

Co-Founder & Strategic Advisor

YegaTech

Uh, so I'm Nitzan. I'm doing a PhD research on AI within architecture and urban design, and I'm also in my background an architect.

and I was tasked in Henning Glassen to understand how do we even tackle this idea of AI? How do we even approach it? When I arrived, he was like, listen, we need some AI. We don't really know how, um, and the first thing that kind of happens in a lot of European companies is we look for governance. We look for, OK, we want to use AI. What are the rules? What are the guardrails? And quite quickly we understood that the way that we want to approach it is not through governance, but it's through culture.

But why culture

uh as opposed to governance, there's many reasons I'm optimistic to try and squeeze all of them into this presentation, but one of them is that there's a lot of aspects about using AI that are not governable because the real conversation is not about AI. It's about the use of data and how we use data, how we utilize data, how we kind of um talk about data to justify some of the decisions that we're making are not something that you can govern.

Uh, we talk a lot about data-driven design, but when data is actually driving the design, is it something that we even want and to what extent, again, all of those things are much more relying on the culture that we developed around data rather than the kind of guardrails and guidelines.

One example of this is AI is based on a training.

of data sets. And we use a lot of non-empirical language in architecture. We use a lot of ambiguous and kind of interpretable language, but if we ask a data-driven mechanism to make a beautiful building, we're much more likely to get something like this than we are to get something like this. Uh, and this is a data-driven uh situation. And it just to show that data-driven is not necessarily always the right thing that we want to do, and the choice again is a cultural one.

Another one is this idea of working with statistics, if we were to be a statistics-based industry, we would probably not design any handicap ramps because statistically there's not enough people on wheelchair to justify the effort and time and money that is being put on this, but we are not just designing for the majority. We're designing for everybody.

Um, another thing is this idea of accountability. We will always be accountable for our own actions on foreign own output. We can never move the accountability into AI and therefore governance around AI is not necessarily something that should be the driving force of adoption.

Um

our approach at Henning Glassen for AI, we also have principles like the previous presentation. We don't want AI to be locked in an ivory tower. We don't have a small department of AI experts that kind of do all the AI work, learn, take all the AI tasks, develop all the AI capabilities, but we're looking at a collective learning, uh, bottom up, upscaling approach where all of the employees in the company are part of this AI transition.

Uh, like I mentioned, this horizontal and vertical adoption horizontal is what everyone in the company can do with AI. Eliana is going to extend on this a little bit, and the vertical is the actual kind of edge cases the champions of AI who are looking to push the boundaries of what AI can do for us.

Uh, on the horizontal aspect, we actually let all of our employees at Henninglasen try out existing AI tools

as much as they want, and then evaluate how much did this benefit your work, how valuable was this for you? Um, how useful, how effective. We gathered all this data and we actually found existing tools that are very, very good for us directly. These are some of the tools that we're using. You can take a photo of this. Uh, and this is the one that you want to take a photo of. These are four principles for AI adoption. We work with literacy, transparency, agency, and accountability.

Literacy means that we don't teach people how to use AI. We actually don't think it's a very valuable thing to teach people what prompt to use and what program to use. We teach people what AI is. We teach them about mechanics of LLM. We teach them about fundamentals of machine learning, and when they get to understand what the technology is, they become comfortable with trying it out, and they also try the right technology for the right problems.

I would love to elaborate on this more. We don't have enough time. Transparency means that we're always open about the use of AI internally and externally. When you don't have a culture of transparency, people use AI without telling that they've used AI. We don't have the internal tools to evaluate something properly. If I don't know if AI was used or not used and also developing this alignment with clients and collaborators about what AI was used for and why.

Agency is an interesting thing, and Ashghan is going to elaborate on this a little bit further. Um, agencies about how much agency do we maintain on our own thing? Of course we can automate things. We can hand over to AI. A lot of the tasks that we do, but we lose touch with our project, and in architecture, specifically as opposed to other sectors, there is an importance of me being actually hands on on the project rather than just optimizing and automating everything to deliver value and speed.

And then of course accountability is more of a mindset. Whatever we produce is on us.

--- SUMMARY ---

Summary

The "AI Implementation" session at AI in AEC 2026 brought together industry leaders to explore the transformative role of artificial intelligence in architecture, engineering, and construction (AEC). Featuring speakers such as Brecht Pierreux, Ashkan Rezaee, Nitsan Bartov, Erik Stroemberg, Max Levander, Jeff Siegel, Vesa Järvinen, Joaquin Arocena, Abel Van Steenweghen, Eliana Nigro, and Jesse Nyberg, the session delved into strategy, leadership, culture, and partnerships. The opening presentation showcased collaboration among 18 U.S. state DOTs, supported by TRB, AASHTO, and ITS America, with Jesse Nyberg sharing findings from peer exchanges hosted by Caltrans and MassDOT. Key themes included governance, workforce readiness, ethics, security, data management, and procurement challenges, exemplified by Texas DOT’s AI strategic plan and Massachusetts DOT’s integration of data across its four divisions.

Max Levander outlined a CEO playbook for AI transformation, emphasizing strategic intent, ownership, timing, and portfolio management. Henning Larsen’s approach combined horizontal and vertical adoption, cultural principles like literacy, transparency, agency, and accountability, and tools such as RAM GPT. Partnerships, including CREA for AI visualization, enhanced adoption efforts, while employee-driven experimentation fostered innovation. Erik Stroemberg highlighted change management at HLP Lighting Design, focusing on metrics like time, effort, quality, and value alignment, supported by AI champions and organizational structures. Copilot-generated lighting designs illustrated practical applications, blending creativity and efficiency.

Proyectos and Macadamia Labs demonstrated AI integration in engineering workflows, deploying solutions for quality assurance, vendor comparison, and technical documentation. Metrics revealed 54 users and 24 repeat users, with Proyectos transitioning from selling hours to fixed pricing models. Engineers co-designed workflows, ensuring trust and usability, while UX iterations refined tools like vendor comparison platforms. Plant 3D integration enabled direct QA checks, streamlining processes and reducing errors. The session underscored the importance of continuous learning, collaboration, and adaptation in leveraging AI’s potential to reshape the AEC industry.

--- SUMMARY ---

Summary

The "AI in AEC 2026" event's "Session 2: Demos" brought together industry leaders to showcase transformative AI applications in architecture, engineering, and construction. Alexey Serous opened the session by demonstrating how Autodesk Robot Structural Analysis software integrates AI tools to automate tasks like modifying trusses and analyzing load-bearing frames. He delved into technical aspects, explaining token-based AI response times, attention layers in large language models (LLMs), and challenges like hallucinations in AI outputs, which require careful context management to ensure reliability.

Bentley's presentation, led by Daniel Eng and Louis Martin Losier, introduced Bentley Copilot and Python Assistant, tools designed to streamline workflows and enhance productivity. Bentley Copilot assists users in navigating software and ensuring compliance with standards like FDOT CAD guidelines, while Python Assistant enables engineers to automate tasks using natural language scripting. These tools simplify dimensioning, drawing production, and onboarding processes, reducing friction in daily workflows.

Peter Madlener showcased Victor, a platform empowering engineers to create, connect, and share Python-based apps via the Victor App Store. He demonstrated features like 3D geometry visualization and seamless integration with local and cloud-based software, emphasizing transparency, collaboration, and the ability to orchestrate workflows across projects.

Kerkko Niemi and Rasmus Paatsalo presented an AI assistant for product selection in construction, focusing on tasks like IFC file parsing, EPD document analysis, carbon calculation optimization, and CSV report generation. They highlighted the importance of storing workflows as reusable skills, enabling engineers to improve efficiency and reduce redundancy across projects.

Richard Parker concluded the session with ADOT Engineer, an AI-native platform designed to automate engineering calculations and generate high-quality scientific reporting outputs. The platform incorporates a robust peer review process, ensuring verified tools and workflows that maintain transparency and accountability. By connecting verified calculations with project data, ADOT Engineer empowers engineers to focus on higher-order design challenges while automating repetitive tasks.

The session underscored AI's transformative potential in AEC, addressing challenges like manual workflows, data silos, and compliance issues. With tools like Bentley Copilot, Victor, and ADOT Engineer, engineers are equipped to save time, enhance collaboration, and deliver projects with greater precision and efficiency.

Now, let's start with the reality of our current workflow right now, structural engineering exists in silos. On one side, we have the big models. In the middle, we have the physics and calculation engines and on the other side we have the project data.

The problem is that these environments are disconnected.

To breach them, we act as human routers. We manually export BDF or an Excel sheet from one tool and retype those values into another. The static file exchange is the bottleneck of our industry.

Every time we reenter a parameter, we aren't just losing time. We are creating an opportunity for human error.

and ensuring that our data becomes stale the moment architectural model changes.

We've all been there. You build the perfect grasshopper script or Python tool, and it works beautifully until the software updates or the project requirements shift by 5%. This is the rigidity problem because these connections are hardcoded. They act as bridle bridges. They don't bend, they break.

We find ourselves in the automation trap.

where the engineers spent more time debugging legacy scripts than actually performing engineering design.

We accidentally traded our engineering hours for maintenance

uh permanently

Even when our scripts do work, they often hit the scalability wall.

Most automation to die today, relies on static logic.

Fixed if X, then y, that's built for one specific scenario.

The moment you need to swap a calculation engine or incorporate a new data source yours back to square one.

rewriting code

This creates the project silos because this integrations aren't aware of each other. The automation we built for Project A is ready, usable for both Project B. We aren't building knowledge base. We're building one of tools that die when the project ends.

And finally, we have the data bottleneck

We are still stuck in a relay race of heavy static files passing back and forth. Every time we export the file, we lose granularity, we lose version control, and we definitely lose uh ability for real-time collaboration.

This file burden is what prevents us from scaling.

To fix this, we need to move away from the spiritual tool dependent links toward a software neutral core where the data, not the file, is the source of truth.

Now in the old world, automation was brittle script. If the tool updated, the script broke.

In the new world, we use orchestration. We have a coordinator, a digital project manager that looks at an agent registry of specialists.

Now instead of writing a fixed path, the coordinator identifies the expertise needed for our specific intent and hires those agents on the fly.

This makes the system resilient. You can swap out a calculation engine in the registry without rewriting a single line of your workflow. And this also makes the dynamic workflows possible.

Now, as I said earlier, traditional ASA industry relies on this relay arrays manually passing heavy overseas, excels and be PDFs, but this is no longer a good option if we want to create dynamic workflows using agents. Now, we are replacing that fragmented workflow with the PCMS.

which stands for the Building Content Management System. Think of it as a shared context context. Every agent sits around this round table. And unlike staticI file, the PCMS is granular. It tracks individual parts and the versions of the pin model in a database.

To interact with it, we use MCP, a universal handshake that allows an AI to browse the database dynamically. It fetches only what is needed

task attended, no more, no less.

And finally, the LLM acts as the bridge. It takes the human command, no matter how it's delivered and translates into precise data queries needed to fuel the machine. It maps the data into our engineering tools and most importantly, it closes the loop. It takes the raw output and pushes it back into the PCMS. And now this is the part where engineers stops being a beta entry clerk and starts being the designed pilot supervising.

--- SUMMARY ---

Summary

The "Multi-Agent AI and Workflows" session at AI in AEC 2026 brought together industry leaders to discuss the transformative role of AI in architecture, engineering, and construction. Kalle Valkama set the stage with Adam Smith's historical insights on labor, emphasizing AI's potential to redefine economic paradigms. Petri Kasari from SVECO Finland showcased their Azure-based multi-agent platform, which integrates BIM software with Microsoft and Autodesk tools to streamline workflows. The platform employs modular agents—Project, Document, and Instruction—to retrieve data, generate documents, and ensure consistency. Kasari highlighted the role of Azure Functions and semantic kernel in orchestration while addressing challenges like data normalization, latency, and adaptability. Jani Nevalainen from AINS Group introduced automated engineering workflows, including a validation agent for quality assurance, environmental impact evaluation, and modular design optimization. Mona Muehlich from Fraunhofer presented a pseudonymization agent and ontology mapper for extracting structured knowledge graphs from meeting protocols, enabling real-time process optimization and conflict detection. Kim Nyberg and Henri Pitkänen from Trimble demonstrated their Agentic platform, featuring tools like a floor plan to 3D model converter, SketchUp's AI-driven 3D mesh creation, and rendering capabilities. A case study with Hensel Phelps illustrated substantial cost savings through automated submittal reviews. The session highlighted AI's ability to enhance productivity, bridge talent gaps, and enable dynamic, scalable workflows, offering a glimpse into the future of AEC innovation.

So next presentation explainable AI for real-time building compliance and design standards in BEAM. So we have Boris Levin and Tom Pattito, co-founders of, uh, uh, Arun.

So you are this Hello everyone. Thank you for joining. Unfortunately, uh, boys couldn't make it, so I will have to entertain you.

Um, so today we will talk about how we use AI to turn late stage compliance to real-time design intelligence, a guidance inside BIM.

As you all know, regularity is national codes are expanding and tightening climate requirements are becoming more demanding and detailed, and liability is shifting towards designers and complexity is just increasing.

but yet we still treat regulation as something we check after design decisions are made, static external checks.

and this mismatch is becoming unbearable. In every building project, compliance sits as the barrier between architectural intentions, obtain and obtainable permitting, but the problem is timing and bandwidth.

We design first and we check compliance only after. It's like printing a chapter or a book and checking for typos after you print.

This is the root cause of most delays, review cycles and permitting blockers.

So compliance today, unfortunately relies on multiple costly consultants, each one reviewing a fragment of the regulations and not all of them in different times.

There isn't one consultant that knows all the regulations at once.

Um, and ultimately, the architects are expected to make sure that intent compliance execution, and timing align, and this becomes, they become the mediators of the entire process, translating clients' ambitions into buildable design, aligning with regulations, coordinating multiple consultants.

and still upholding their own design vision and office standards.

So the whole process today is human dependent, fragile architects designed first, consultants review later. Authorities respond last.

So of course, uh

this process creates long feedback loops, weeks of interpretation, multiple redesign cycles, permit rejections and delays, and a lot of costs for the industry.

Just an example, one residential project after almost a year of planning, the team discovered only a single definition in a distinct master plan, the technical roof counted as a full floor.

4 apartments disappeared overnight. Mills in clients' revenue were gone, and it wasn't a design mistake. It was just a mis-sentence in a PDF, but these mistakes were invisible during the design process.

but compliance already changed elsewhere. For example, in banking, AI-driven fintech monitors transactions continuously in flags suspicious behavior in real time as well in the health industry and legal tech, but architecture um stayed behind and this industries in banking and legal rules are complex, are non-negotiable, um, but it shifted from post review to real-time guidance.

So still, architecture, although the stakes are high, the rules are dense, and mistakes are costly, we're still anchored to lead stage reviews and manual feedback.

Why LLMs don't solve it alone.

Architecture is special. It requires measuring life geometry, understanding relationships between elements, understanding floor to floor conditions, providing cetacean and traceability, and much more. They are extraordinary attack

but compliance is not a Q&A exercise.

Geometry matters. This is not a language problem. It's a geometry and context problem. Buildings are special systems and regulations are conditional, spatial logic. LLMs alone hallucinate and work on predictability, but answers must be deterministic, parametric, explainable, and geometry aware.

So we know that compliance checks should be based on geometry in context, not as a checklist, but as a continuous reasoning layer. Always present, always aware of geometry, always aligned with the regulation.

So the real question is how do we embed regulation in the design environment itself inside the workflow.

The answer is an AI native compliance engine embedded directly inside beam tools working constantly in the background on live ongoing developed models.

It's like having one consultant

who knows each and every regulation, has seen thousands of models and and edge cases and continuously monitoring the BIM model in real time.

When it, when a design decision creates a potential issue, this engine flags the flags it in the model within a few seconds. Explain why it matters with broad cross model context, cites the relevant regulation with RRref and guides you through actionable planning alternatives. The architect remains in control, responsibility stays human. AI only provides continuous visible reasoning.

To make that possible, we had to build a compounded compliance intelligence tack orchestrated by a multi-agent workflow that understands geometry, context, and building codes simultaneously. Multiple layers working together depending on one another.

At the foundation, we formalized human regulations, converted to computable logic and rules.

Then we move to beam interpretation, interprets complex, messy, ever changing beam models.

and then on top of that hybrid reasoning engine that combines rules, geometry and context to evaluate this unconditions, and at the end, of course, real-time validation continuously inside BIM.

Perhaps the bigger gains won't come from doing the same thing come from doing the same tasks faster, but from deleting entire legacy steps bridging between design actions and reviews. AI is enabling a system that is acting as a catalyst for everyone involved in the project, the developers, consultants, architects, and the authorities.

To unlock this potential, we had to address 3 core challenges. First, regulations are not machine ridable by default. Building codes were never written for machines. They were written by humans for humans. So turning regularity texts and sketches into something a system can reason on requires interpretation, spatial obstruction, engineering judgment, and symbolic logic.

not just training a model, but an accurate, inaccurate regularity reasoning layer.

Secondly, B models are not clean and standardized datasets.

Beam models are inconsistent. They're messy. They're not, they're firm specific. They're often incomplete, and they reflect design intention more than technical correctness, understanding a B model means knowing what a corridor or an exit is usable space or structure when a room tag is missing or what is an annotation or an actual element.

Most generic AI tools can see shapes, but to reason an architecture they must understand geometry, typology, hierarchy, use and constraint, and all of these interconnected or often conflicting.

And lastly, trust and responsibility are non-negotiable. In architecture. Compliance decisions must be explainable, traceable, and defensible. Black box outputs simply cannot cross that boundary, an AI system guiding compliance decisions must defend its reasoning by pinpointing the issue.

highlighted on the model and citing the regulation in an accurate way.

When did the system that connects AI to regulations, beam data, and human responsibility in real time. Once regularity, ambiguity, messy beam data, and rust are just together with AI, leveraging those capabilities, the right way becomes possible.

So looking back for decades, drawings were just lines on paper. Beam change something fundamental. Models became organized, measurable, and data rich. Walls have structures, doors have properties. Rooms have areas and quantities, buildings became computable.

but compliance, it didn't evolve with it. We still export 2D drawings with still check PDFs and we still review them manually or externally to the design process itself.

So A alone doesn't easily solve compliance. Neither does beam alone self-compliance, but by tapping directly inside the beam model using it as live context for eugenic workflows, the process becomes an ongoing conversation between design intent

--- SUMMARY ---

Summary

The "AI Technology and Systems" session at AI in AEC 2026 showcased transformative applications of artificial intelligence in architecture, engineering, and construction (AEC), emphasizing scalability, sustainability, and intelligent workflows. Featuring speakers Boris Levin, Tom Batito, Christopher Mouflard, Aisan Sharifian, Tero Järvinen, Sonja Oksanen, Tommi Arola, and Otto Alhava, the session explored how AI-driven systems can address fragmented documentation, enhance decision-making, and streamline processes across diverse use cases.

Tommi Arola and Tero Järvinen opened with a discussion on knowledge graphs and Finnish nomenclature standardization, highlighting their role in enriching BIM models with product data. They introduced the scalable Model Context Protocol (MCP) server, which links structured data to AI systems, and emphasized the PEPPOL messaging system for automating procurement processes. Ontologies in construction and CSRD reporting were addressed as critical components for financial departments and sustainability goals.

Boris Levin and Tom Batito presented an AI-driven compliance engine embedded in BIM tools, offering real-time validation and guidance on building standards. This explainable AI solution reduces permitting delays and costly rework, showcasing the scalability of hybrid systems in compliance workflows.

Aisan Sharifian focused on integrating AI with Heritage BIM (HBIM) for sustainable rehabilitation of 20th-century social housing. She highlighted the New European Bauhaus (NEB) program and the importance of predictive frameworks that incorporate human factors, energy metrics, and cultural values. Semantic segmentation and digital twins were discussed as essential tools for long-term facility management and sustainability.

Christopher Mouflard concluded with a comparative study of convolutional neural networks (CNNs) and vision-language models (VLMs) for object detection in 2D construction drawings. He demonstrated how hybrid systems, incorporating voting mechanisms and Intersection over Union (IOU) metrics, can improve accuracy and efficiency in analyzing complex architectural documents. The persistent reliance on 2D drawings in construction was emphasized as a critical challenge for AI integration.

The session underscored the transformative potential of AI in structuring, interpreting, and applying information across AEC workflows. By leveraging intelligent systems like the Anthropic AI model, scalable MCP server, and hybrid AI frameworks, the industry can achieve greater collaboration, efficiency, and sustainability in its processes.

Construction Networks survey, we're seeing this on the ground. So 40% of teams are starting to allocate AI budgets, and then 38% are now starting to create implementation teams. So teams on the ground that are focused on rolling out AI in a safe and secure way.

You need to understand where AI literacy is and where AI adoption is today. Number 2 is to map personus. So walking through step one here. Our first step is a survey, and I'm gonna walk you through the questions that we had. First question was simple. How often do you currently use AI? That's really valuable just to quickly understand kind of the AI literacy level or the capability level of your organization.

Number 2 was, do you use AI as part of your work today. A lot of people might be using AI tools, but they might not feel comfortable to use it in your work context. So we wanted to understand the differences between these two answers.

Number 3, what we wanted to also understand is what is the biggest concern about using AI at work. A lot of people don't want to use AI work for a variety of reasons, and any training you need to do needs to handle those objections effectively. And then finally, what topics would you like to learn most about? So for us to gain adoption and to gain kind of

people actually using AI, it needs to be relevant and contextualized to their project context or to their specific use cases. And what I found was really interesting in the results.

Number one, we had all these concerns around losing their jobs and stealing data. People were actually very, very scared about using chat GPT or any other AI model at work because they were scared that they'd do a misstep. It'd be released to the media, which happened quite often in working in public authorities, and they lose their jobs. So we needed to address that proactively. We also realized that people just weren't sure they were allowed to, even though we'd had comms saying, you can use tooling, this is how to use it.

People still didn't know that how to use it

And then finally, one of the biggest challenges is where can I learn? Even if you care about AI, there actually isn't very many resources to go out there and land. There's a few people online, but you need to know about them. And inside your organization, you don't know, should I talk to the innovation team? Should I talk to the IT team? Who actually owns AI and who can I go to for help?

So based off this, what we did is we mapped an AI capability pyramid. And what I'm going to do is I'm gonna walk you through each level of this pyramid, and then how we developed training so that people could be increased in the skill level across this particular pyramid.

So we found that kind of 90 to 95% of our organization was an AI novice. And what an AI novice was, was someone who's actually never used AI before or was kind of scared and apprehensive due to security and data risks, and more than that, they weren't actually technical. They didn't necessarily understand the software backing behind this.

So what we did is we created training for them all around an AI crash course. So and a 101 to get started. And what we did is we would get them in a room, we'd get them to sign up to Chat GPT and we would get them to actually start using it with guided exercises, so they could see the value of how it works. You want people to have the tools in their hand, using it in a safe and secure way so that we reduce risk. And before we do that, we had some really quick theory. Number one was what is A

I and how does it work? You put in a prompt, you get information back, and what you write changes the answer. People needed to start to understand that.

Number 2 was addressing some concerns around strengths and limitations, so hallucination is one of the biggest concerns that we see people having. And if AI hallucinates, they stop using it. But we explained to them how it hallucinates, how to use a human in the loop system, but also understanding it's just a tool. It's a new tool in your tool set, and you need to know when and where to use it. If you don't know where to use it, you're not going to get adoption.

And then finally, we had to share our company guidelines. You know, how do we approach AI? What is safe and secure use? What are you allowed to upload and what you can't upload. And then finally, what we also do is we show the capability areas. So we mapped 4 different capability areas. We created guided tasks, demos, and exercises so that each individual in our workshop could walk through, start experimenting, and start to learn how to use AI, and all we want to do

with this cohort of people is to make them understand, make them to have the confidence to go, I can experiment with AI. I feel like I have an AI mandate, and I feel safe. I feel secure. I won't lose my job because I'm following the rules. And we clearly explained that to people.

After that, what we did is we had the AI explorers. So this is a cohort of people who I have some experience with AI tools, and they understand the basic concepts, but they haven't yet actually grained real value from it. And what I mean by that, it's not actually as a part of their daily workflow. So if we think back to some of the earlier presentations today, until you're starting to use it on a daily basis. You're not actually starting to see the productivity improvements that we want. So what

we started to do is we asked ourselves the question of how do we move from experimentation to habit, and the way we did that is by addressing some of our survey concerns of, you know, what do you want to use AI for. So we created use case specific training.

First was AI for writing. So how do you use it for emails? How do you use it to write reports? And how do you use it to improve clarity, especially our cohort of people. We had a large number of employees who used English as a second language, so helping them to write faster, helping them to review their work that is written by AI and giving them a process around doing that and training them, help them to feel safe in using it, but also understand when and where to use it.

The second one we do, which I think is really relevant, is also AI for Excel. So it's a very popular tool, of course, but we taught them how to generate formulas, um, and also what they can and can't upload. So very important to understand how to anonymize data before you upload it, because we were using free tools. We didn't have any commercial licenses at these points. So just teaching people the understanding around AI for Excel was incredibly valuable.

And then finally, at this stage is when we started to do what we call prompting 101. So for the explorer the novice cohort, we don't teach them about prompting, cause they don't understand, um, when you're experimenting, that prompting actually creates changes. But at this level where you've started to use AI, you understand the variability of output. You can teach different prompting frameworks, and we developed our own what we call the stingray model of how to work in a human in the loop way to get faster outputs

from AI. So at this level, we start doing prompting, and our entire goal here was to turn what was we saw as occasional use with AI into a very consistent workflow habit, so that people every time they open Excel, every time they stopped writing, they know exactly how to use AI, when to use it, and how to feel safe in using AI without leaking data, um, accidentally.

Now, the next phase and kind of what was exciting for us is the idea of an AI practitioner or what our term for what we saw as an AI power user. These are the people who regularly use AI, and it's embedded in their workflows. But the challenge and the truth is not everyone's going to be at this level. It tailors very quickly from novice to explorer to practitioner. It's the people who are curious and excited about using AI. For this group of people, we wanted them

to become power users. So we started to give really tailored training for them. First, we went to Business Lipecific. So a lot of them would be in specific business teams. We would survey them and create process maps of, hey, how do you do your work today? We then um understand how to integrate AI into that process map, so 20 steps to 5 steps or 6 steps using various tools, and we trained the entire team in these processes, using the people who are champions to, to showcase and validate what we were

saying.

We also created communities at this point in time, so small groupings of people who were really excited by AI, but they felt alone in the organization. and people would come to us and ask us questions. what we start to notice is after about 3 months, we started to have enough people that they became AI explorers. --- SUMMARY ---

Summary

At the AI in AEC 2026 event, the keynote session "From Curiosity to Capability: Upskilling Teams for AI Adoption in Construction," presented by Simon Oster and Bhragan Paramanantham, explored strategies for overcoming barriers to AI adoption in the construction industry. Bhragan, a civil engineer with experience at AECOM and government infrastructure innovation, shared his expertise in designing AI training programs tailored to industry-specific challenges. He highlighted the AI adoption gap, citing Placer Solutions statistics that revealed 74% of individuals use tools like ChatGPT and Claude regularly, yet 68% of companies remain unprepared for AI-driven disruption. The Dodge Construction Network survey underscored fears of data leaks, with over 10% of users accidentally exposing sensitive information, a critical concern in industries handling confidential project data and intellectual property.

To address these challenges, Bhragan introduced a five-step AI capability uplift framework, emphasizing assessment, persona mapping, literacy training, dynamic adaptation, and fostering citizen developers. He detailed the AI capability pyramid, categorizing users into novices, explorers, practitioners, and innovators, with tailored training programs for each group. Novices received crash courses on safe AI use, while explorers were trained in use-case-specific applications like AI for writing, Excel, and prompting techniques using the Stingray model. Practitioners, or power users, were empowered through business-line-specific process mapping, AI office hours, and community-building initiatives like Viva Engage and the AI bulletin.

The session also addressed cultural resistance in government organizations, referencing the Australian satire "Utopia" to illustrate challenges such as fear of job loss and slow adoption rates. Bhragan shared how executive assistants played a pivotal role in gaining leadership buy-in by showcasing the practical benefits of AI adoption. He outlined the innovation team's structure, which included idea generation, R&D, and scaling efforts to prioritize high-ROI use cases. The concept of capability overhang was introduced, highlighting how AI innovation, including tools like AI agents, is outpacing organizations' ability to adopt new technologies.

By identifying and nurturing power users, organizations can bridge this gap and accelerate adoption. The session concluded with a call to action, urging attendees to invest in structured training programs, empower AI champions, and leverage platforms like Viva Engage to drive adoption across their teams. As Bhragan emphasized, while AI's future is already here, its benefits will only be fully realized by those who prioritize capability-building and responsible use.

--- SUMMARY ---

Summary

The session "Impact of Agentic AI in AEC Value Chain," part of the AI in AEC 2026 event, brought together panelists Annina Peisa, Kalle Valkama, Sam Saatchi, Kim Nyberg, Sam Zolfagharian, and Simon Oster to discuss the transformative role of AI in architecture, engineering, and construction (AEC). Panelists shared their varying levels of enthusiasm for AI, ranging from cautious optimism to high excitement, and highlighted proprietary tools like Sweco GPT and Arcadis GPT, which ensure secure data handling and governance. The pyramid of AI adoption was introduced, categorizing firms from reactive to strategic innovators, emphasizing the importance of data quality and governance in driving AI adoption.

Examples of AI-powered innovations, such as flood simulations, tendering, and groundwater calculations, showcased the technology's potential to enhance construction and maintenance processes. Predictions for advancements like large vision models, proprietary AI systems, and energy-efficient infrastructure were discussed as pivotal developments by 2030. The panel underscored the importance of agile workflows and cross-functional teams in reshaping AEC delivery models, while collaborative opportunities between startups, academia, and software vendors were deemed essential for progress.

Challenges such as disconnected data, outdated systems, and the risk of agent sprawl were highlighted, with AI seen as a tool to address client trust and expectations. The discussion also touched on balancing headcount with token costs and the role of AI in democratizing knowledge and enabling PhD-level assistance. Sequoia Capital's prediction about trillion-dollar companies underscored the transformative potential of AI, while GitHub Copilot was cited as a successful example of AI adoption, enhancing productivity across teams.

Despite concerns about energy consumption and job displacement, the panel remained optimistic about AI's ability to revolutionize the AEC industry. The session concluded with a call for collaboration across the value chain to ensure AI's benefits are fully realized, setting the stage for a future where AI drives efficiency, quality, and innovation in AEC.

Good morning everyone and thank you for being here. My name is Babake Hassani, and I am a PhD researcher in Istanbul Ein University in Turkey, and today I will present my research title Data to even Special Optimization, Smart building using digital to feedback loops and AI simulation.

This research explore how digital technology and artificial intelligence can help building adopt to constantly changing needs of their occupants. In particular, the study investigates how digital twin systems combined with AI simulation can support dynamic spatial optimization inside the smart buildings.

To begin, I would like to highlight a fundamental paradox in architecture. Most buildings are designed to last for 40 to 50 years. However, the way we actually used this building changed much more quickly.

Work pattern, organizational structure, collaboration style, and even technological tools evolve every few years. As a result, a space that we're optimized during the design age often become inefficient or uncomfortable over time.

In other words, we design building as a static object, but human activity inside them are highly dynamic.

When problems emerge, and we usually attribute them to mechanical systems such as HA, uh, performance or lightning condition.

However, in many cases or many scenarios, and the real issue is not the building uh system themselves, but rather than a special configuration of the environment.

Here this raises an important question How can building become more responsive to changing human needs.

This question formed the starting point of this research. The objective of the study is to explore how building uh evolved from a static container into adaptive environments.

In instead of treating a special design as a one-time decision made during the design phase, we proposed a system where a special configuration can be continuously evaluated and improved throughout the building life cycle.

To achieve this, the research introduced a framework that integrates two emerging technology.

digital twin system and generative artificial intelligence. The key idea is the close this loop, feedback loop between the real world building data and computional spatial optimization.

Let us now look at the proposed framework.

The architecture of the system is organized into 3 interconnected layer. The first layer is a physical layer and this layer represent the actual building environments, including IoT sensors, installed throughout the whole building.

These sensors collect the real-time data about environmental and behavioral conditions such as occupancy level, uh, acoustic condition and also thermal comfort. And the second layer is digital tin. digital to act as a real-time virtual representing of the building. It continuously receive and process data from the physical environment and update digital model accordingly.

In other words, it functioned as a dynamic mirror of the building.

The third layer is AI agent, which acts as an analytical and decision making component of the system.

within this layer, we apply deep reinforcement learning techniques to simulate potential spatial change and evaluate their performance.

Throughout this architecture, the system becomes capable of analyzing spatial conditions and exploring alternative configuration confusionally.

A core innovation of the proposed framework is lies in what we call digital digital twin.

and feedback loops. In many current implement, digital training system function primarily as a just like monitoring dashboard.

A visualize environmental data, but they do not actively participate in decision making.

In contrast, our framework transfer the digital twin into active optimizing system.

The AI agent continuously evaluated a special condition and task a simple but powerful question.

What would happen if the special layout were adjusted.

To answer this question, and the system runs thousands of simulations within digital environments, different spatial scenarios are tested, including variation in furniture arrangement, zoning the strategy and circulation pattern.

Because all these experiments occur uh inside the digital model.

they can perform without disrupting the real building environment and through this

iterative simulation process, the system can identify configuration that improve both environmental performance and occupant comfort.

From a technical perspective, the framework bridged the gap between the building information modeling and artificial intelligence. The geometry and the special structure of the building is is represented using BIM model created in a rabbit, which provides a static geometry and a special relationship required for simulation. Python is scripting is used to manage the data pipeline integrated sensory information and control the s

im ul ation process. The system is deployed on a cloud-based infrastructure using Microsoft Azure, which enable real-time data ingestion escapable storage and continuous integration of IoT sensors, a stream within the digital twin environment.

Finally, the machine learning component is implemented using tensor flow where reinforcement learning agencies trained to evaluate and optimize a spatial configuration. Together, these tech technology create an inter workflow is beach building data, a special model, and I, I algorithm operate within a unified confusional environments.

Before applying uh AI simulation, it's important to understand what type of data drive the system. The simulation model relies on a multiple data source. These include occupancy pattern, environmental condition, a special contrast and behavioral usage data. Together these data sets allow the system to replicate the real operation conditional inside the building. This data-driven foundation enabled the AI agent to evaluate a special perform

ance more accurately

At this point, the important question arise is AI-based special optimization actually feasible in real architectural practices. To explore this question, we can examine one of the most influential industry example in this field. The project is Autodesk Mars's office in Toronto.

In this project, Autodesk applied generative design techniques to optimize a special layout of their office environment. As you watch these short videos, notice how design process is treated not simply as a drawing plan, but a solving a computional optimizing problem. In this project, Ardes collected detailed information from approximately 250 employees and they collected data, including several types of spatial preference

for example, adjustments in relationships and identify which team needs collaborative for collaborate frequently and they also called uh collected information about acoustic preference and distinguishing between employees who required a question environment and those working in a collaborative settings. Additionally, daylight access and environmental comfort preference for included in a dataset. All these

parameters were translated into Comfucional constraint and objective.

Using the parameter and the generative design produced approximately 10,000 different spatial configuration. Each configuration was evaluated against multiple preference sectaria. The algorithm had to balance competing goals such as special density, access to daylight, privacy requirements, and collaborative proximity. These process demonstrates how artificial intelligence can explore uh design space for

larger what it typically possible through a manual design iteration. The result is a set of optimized spatial layouts generated through computional exploration. However, these optimization happened before the building was actually used.

However, this example also revealed an important limitation, the optimization process in Autodesk project occurred only once before the office was built. After construction, the special layouts remained relatively fixed and our research proposed the next step in this evolution. In a set of performing the spatial optimization only during the design files, we propose a system capable of continuous optimization throughout the

building life cycle and by by integrating digital twin and AI simulation, the system can repeatedly evaluate a spatial condition as a organizational pattern evolved.

This enabled building to respond to change in occupancy, work pattern, and environmental condition over time.

Simulation result and

to test feasibility of this approach, we conducted a predictive simulator based on a typical open plan floor office. Within the simulation environment, uh, the AI agent evaluate multiple spatial configuration while considering environmental conditions and occupant dis distribution based on simulated 3 months evaluation period in a controlled digital tin environment. The results suggested several

potential improvements compared to the baseline configuration. The results indicate notable improvement in energy performance and occupant comfort. This improvement was primarily associated with the optimizing spatial density in relation to daylight availability. Although these results are based on the simulation, they suggest that spatial optimization can significantly, significantly influence both environmental performance

and user satisfaction

Definitive promising result, several challenges remain. One of the most important issue concern about humanity, human agency.

Uh, if an algorithm if an algorithm suggests moving furniture or reorganizing the spatial zone, how much control should occupants have over these decisions. Our finding indicate that the system should not operate as a fully automated mechanism. In a set, it should function as a decision support system for facility manager and designers. In this model, the AI provides recommend

ation and analytical insight while human stakeholder retain final decision-making authority.

To conclude the future of this smart building may not depend solely on improving mechanical systems or energy technology equally important if ability of the building to adopt specialty to human needs by integrating digital twin feedback loops with AI-driven simulation, we can move toward the new generation of built environments

uh, that's continuously learned from data and evolve over time. Such adaptive environments have a potential to improve energy efficiency, special performance, and occupants well-being simultaneously. To wrap up, we believe that the true intelligence of the smart buildings lies on, lies not just in a sensors, but its ability to adopt the people inside. --- SUMMARY ---

Summary

The "Smart Buildings and Cities" session at AI in AEC 2026 showcased groundbreaking advancements in AI-driven building optimization, moderated by Henri Pitkänen. Mohammad Seyfi from LUT University presented his work on reinforcement learning in energy management systems, emphasizing the DriveTwix project and Budapest setup powered by Home Assistant platforms. He highlighted the use of Soft Actor-Critic algorithms to optimize energy consumption and thermal comfort, leveraging energy market pricing, step response tests, and linear regression for building modeling. Babak Ehsani from Istanbul Technical University introduced a digital twin framework, integrating Microsoft Azure and TensorFlow for continuous spatial optimization. His research proposed adaptive environments that evolve with occupant needs, referencing Autodesk's Mars Office project, which utilized occupancy patterns and adjacency relationships for generative design. Knut Nordanger from Sintef Community demonstrated a semantic architecture for conversational AI in buildings, grounded in Brick ontology and knowledge graphs. His work at the ZEB Laboratory showcased anomaly detection using sensors like CO2 levels and window status, with actionable recommendations generated via hybrid search and Flux queries. The session underscored AI's transformative role in creating responsive, efficient, and occupant-centric built environments, bridging the gap between static design and dynamic human activity.

--- SUMMARY ---

Summary

The "Climate Technology" session at AI in AEC 2026 highlighted innovative AI applications for addressing environmental challenges. Rania Labib presented her agentic AI workflow for urban heat island (UHI) analysis, focusing on the impact of rising urban temperatures on energy consumption and air quality. Using Houston, Texas—a rapidly urbanizing city with limited canopy coverage—as a case study, Labib demonstrated how NDVI and NDBI indices correlate vegetation and built-up areas with land surface temperature, revealing urbanization's disproportionate effect on heat intensity.

The workflow integrates computer vision and large language models (LLMs) into a multi-agent AI pipeline that automates satellite image acquisition, preprocessing, segmentation (15–30 minutes), tiling (2–2.5 hours), and report generation, reducing analysis time to three hours per city. The Python- and Flask-based dashboard allows users to select cities, adjust parameters, and analyze tiles, producing city-scale reports, temperature distribution graphs, and AI-driven tile classifications. Recommendations for mitigating heat effects include cool roofs, permeable pavements, and vegetation expansion, addressing intensity drivers like asphalt and industrial areas.

Labib emphasized the tool's teaching applications for non-experts, such as students, while audience questions underscored its potential for integrating policy frameworks, supporting real estate development, and saving time compared to manual analysis. She also discussed future work on batch multi-city analysis, multi-temporal studies, and higher-resolution images. Accessible via GitHub, the tool provides raw data for further analysis, making it a valuable resource for academic and practical applications alike.

--- SUMMARY ---

Summary

The eighth session of the AI in AEC 2026 conference, titled "Sustainable Business Models," brought together industry leaders, researchers, and innovators to explore AI's transformative role in architecture, engineering, and construction (AEC). Bart Brink introduced the Netherlands' AI for housing program, which leverages AI to address housing challenges through value cases, mindset frameworks, and ecosystem collaboration. He also highlighted the Build by AI platform, which fosters innovation and federated data sharing across stakeholders.

Petra Svensson Gleisner and Ivana Kildsgaard shared insights from Sweden's Strategic Innovation Program, Smart Built Environment, focusing on generative AI's impact on business models and sustainability. They showcased initiatives like Trafficverket's AI tender, which rewards AI-driven savings, and platforms such as Tailor Made, enabling homebuyers to customize apartments, and the Great Place to Live score, a social sustainability metric for cities.

In Finland, Piia Sormunen and Osku Torro unveiled the AI Champion project, a €20 million initiative to develop AI agents for supply chain management and industrialize construction processes. Complementing this, the Finnish AI Playbook, presented by Tommi Arola, Pieti Marjavaara, and Joonas Lehtovaara, offers practical guidance for integrating AI into workflows, emphasizing data governance, risk management, and ethical considerations. Pavlina Nikolova from Ignite underscored the importance of metadata and classification in organizing construction data, enabling AI to deliver actionable insights and streamline operations.

Speakers emphasized the need for structured data, federated sharing, and scalable solutions to overcome fragmentation and inefficiencies in the AEC sector. They explored diverse business models, including outcome-based approaches, data-driven insights, and AI-enhanced workflows. The session highlighted the importance of aligning technology with business processes, fostering a culture of innovation, and preparing for the twin transitions of sustainability and technological advancement. By leveraging tools like the EU's Digital Product Passport and AI-driven platforms, the industry is poised to unlock new efficiencies and opportunities while addressing pressing societal challenges.

OK, uh, I think it's still morning, so good morning everyone.

and uh welcome back.

Uh, my name is Sergei von Bach. um CEO from BST Architects.

and also uh one of the

supporting organization member.

Uh, we are about to begin a series of presentations exploring how artificial intelligence is reshaping architectural design, planning, and construction workflows.

Its stock brings a new unique perspective on how AI can support early stage decision making

improve efficiency and open new creative possibilities.

Our first, uh, speaker is uh Dylan Richman.

uh, from Bentley Systems, uh, uh, he's a research engineer specializing in AI and optimization for engineering design. He is the author of 5 patent applications.

uh, in this field. Today, he will show us how special priors can guide conceptual site planning and support more informed early stage design. Please welcome Dylan Richman.

OK

Hello everyone. My name is Dylan Wichman. I'm a research engineer at Bentley Systems. Today, I'm presenting predictive design as an alternative to generative design, which is a term I'm sure, uh, many of you are familiar with. So to demonstrate predictive design where're specifically going to look at, uh, conceptual site design and building placement.

But before we get into that, let's talk a bit about generative models. Generative AI has been around for decades, but the recent explosion from of large language models from companies like OpenAI and Anthropic has started to dramatically shift the focus of AI researcher towards these generative approaches.

And it's not just text, we also have image generation models like you could see here where you can create photorealistic images just from simple text prompts.

So this momentum has naturally led people to ask what can we do in our domains with these generative models.

Researchers have started started to answer this question and we've seen generative models being applied to uh floor plan layout, topology optimization and uh site design uh just to name a few.

And many of these models have genuinely impressive results, but once we start to think about how these would actually fit into design workflows, some fundamental issues start to surface.

The first is a role shift. So when a generative workflow, the designer goes from leading the design process to becoming an evaluator. It's a bit like pulling a slot machine. You give the model your context, pull the lever, and you hope the output is usable. If it's not, you pull it again.

Second is constraint satisfaction. Real design problems, as I'm sure many of you know, have many complex and often overlapping constraints and it's very difficult to encode these constraints and feed them into the generative models.

Lastly is search space collapse. Design problems have rich, diverse search spaces, and when we use generative models, we are collapsing these search spaces into individual designs.

So instead of increasing what the designer can consider were actually narrowing it.

So we started to ask the question, how can we give the designer the benefits of predictive design while still allowing them to be in the front seat of the design process and that's where predictive design comes in.

So instead of asking the model to hand us an answer, we're asking it to give us the what the landscape of good answers look like. So say we have a building footprint that we're trying to place in a parcel boundary, and we have an access point at the top.

In a generative approach, the model would probably place the building for you and maybe give you multiple other locations, and then you would be left to actually pick the the location that you best see as fit.

In uh predictive design, we instead give the engineer a feasibility map.

So this is the output from a model that's basically showing the engineer where the most likely spots a building should be placed based on designs that it has seen in the past.

So to build a model that can learn something like this, we obviously need a lot of data.

Specifically, we need a big data set of real world sites. So we first had to define what a site actually is. In our case, we're looking at commercial or industrial parcels that have at least one building, uh, placed on them and may have road access points.

We source uh parcel data from 31 counties across 10 US states, um, and got road and building information from OpenStreetMap.

Uh, the total data set was 21.7 million parcels, um, but after filtering for usable sites we're left with about 575,000.

So how do we go from raw geospatial data to something we can actually feed a machine learning model.

We first identify these relevant commercial and industrial parcels.

extract the parcel boundary from report all.

and get the building footprint and access points from OpenStreetMap and then apply a normalization process which basically allows this to be fed into a machine learning model.

For the model itself, we chose Perceiver IO, which is an architecture introduced by Google DeepMind in 2021. It's a transformer-based architecture that is designed to work with varying modalities, making it perfect for this type of problem.

So we take our raw inputs and then we feed them into an encoder which turns these inputs into tokens that the model can then read. The model processes these tokens and then outputs the feasibility map.

So similar to how LLM's tokenize words. We needed a way to tokenize our geometric inputs, and an interesting problem we encountered was figuring out how to do this.

So points are pretty easy. They have an X and Y coordinate, so they easily map to a fixed link token, but when it comes to polygons, it's a bit more difficult because polygons can have any number of sides.

So our solution is to decompose the polygon into its individual line segments. And since each of these segments consist of two points. They then naturally map onto fixed length tokens just like our point geometries.

So to put our results in a perspective we needed to have a few comparison models.

Uh, the first is a centroid model, which just predicts to place the building in the center of the parcel.

The second is what we call a uniform feasible model. This basically takes all of the locations that the building fits in the parcel and gives them a uniform value. So in this case you can see the ground truth X is actually slightly outside of this region because the building was slightly outside of the parcel geometry.

The last is a UNE model, which is a very common image to image machine learning model.

OK, so let's see how these models compared on a few metrics.

The first metric we looked at is called top constraint. So this basically takes the highest probable location and sees if the building is contained in the parcel or if it's not contained in the parcel. So obviously we want this to be higher. The random feasibility model gets this trivially because it only considers a valid locations. The centroid model leads this, which makes sense because if you place a building in the center of the parcel, the od

d s that it's completely contained are fairly high.

So our model lags behind in this metric, but it doesn't quite tell the whole story. So let's look at the, the next metric, which is ground truth Pixel rank. So this is measuring the ground truth value and seeing how well the or how high the model uh predicts that the, the building should go there.

So it's a test of accuracy of how well the model works, and you can see our model, uh, easily beats all the others in this metric.

The last metric is specificity. So if the model um basically predicts to put the building everywhere, that's not very informative, so we would like the model to um actually pick a specific location and be more specific. So in this case a lower value corresponds to a higher specificity. So in this example on the left, the output is more specific than the one on the right.

And again, uh, our model leads in this metric.

OK, so now let's take a look at what um these maps actually look like side by side. So this is the site we were just looking at. Uh, we can see that the centroid models picking the center, the uniform feasible is predicting this box of feasible locations and then the UNET doesn't do a very great job. It's basically predicting in the center, but it's kind of spread out.

Our model's output, as you will notice, uh, is the best out of all of them. It's accurately identifies where the building was located, um, and is a highly specific output

This next site is pretty interesting because of the way the building is oriented. It can only be placed in the, um, like very thin parts of the like thin center of the site. Um, and the easiest way to see this is by looking at the uniform feasible output.

Again, the you know uh UNEP model doesn't do a great job, and then you can see our model while it does prefer the opposite side of the parcel to place the building actually does a pretty good job, um, identifying all of these um valid locations and ranks them fairly highly.

And we see a uh pretty uh similar behavior with this side as well.

Uh, in this site, there are actually 4 access points on the right side, uh, which in our data set was indicative of a parking lot being there, so the model was able to look at that and figure out that the building should go on the opposite side of the parking lot.

This last site um has the most freedom, degrees of freedom to place the building, um, and you will see again the UNE model doesn't do a great job, um, and our model has identified this center column as the most likely uh place to um place the building and it captures the ground truth location.

And it also has this, uh, notch on the right-hand side, um, which is basically saying, you know, if we're going to put the building off center, it should probably be uh vertically um centered.

OK, so another useful way to look at um this model's output is to watch how the heat map changes as we rotate the building.

Um, so right now we have an access point on the bottom right and uh we're currently predicting to place the building, uh, in the top center.

And then as I start to rotate this building, you'll see how the heat map evolves and once we um have this building uh vertically the model's able to figure out that it should be placed on the left side and not block that access point because we're probably going to put a parking lot, um.

key, um, driver of predictive design is that we don't need this model to learn every rule like we do in generative design because we're working with another model that knows everything, and that model is the engineer. It's the designer.

--- SUMMARY ---

Summary

At AI in AEC 2026, "Session 9: Conceptual Design" brought together experts to explore AI's transformative role in architecture. Sergej von Bagh, CEO of BST Architects, opened the session by emphasizing AI's potential to reshape design, planning, and construction workflows. Dylan Wichman from Bentley Systems introduced predictive design as an alternative to generative design, addressing challenges like role shift, constraint satisfaction, and search space collapse. Using Perceiver IO, he demonstrated feasibility maps for site planning, analyzing a dataset of 575,000 parcels. Examples included parking lot placement and thin center site designs. Generative AI applications such as floor plan layout, topology optimization, and site design were also discussed.

Kubilay Şahinler from Traility presented ARKE Estimate, a tool powered by CatBoost models and normalized qualitative data using LLMs to predict project hours. He emphasized treating data as an organizational asset and shared plans for capacity optimization and early warning systems. Seeja Sudhakaran, a doctoral researcher at the University of West London, showcased her school design prototype, which uses adjacency matrix clustering and evolutionary algorithms to optimize layouts. She highlighted gaps in AI tools for institutional architecture and demonstrated BIM integration for conceptual design, allowing user-defined typologies and spatial adjacencies.

Mauri Laasonen from Tampere University of Applied Sciences explored AI visualization for housing design, showing how simple mass models can generate usable images for early-stage discussions. He noted that AI-generated images, while imperfect, effectively facilitate dialogue among stakeholders. A disclaimer for AI-generated visuals was recommended to address errors. Mikael Johanes from ETH Zurich presented his research on circular fit-outs for office interiors, combining graph representation for spatial configurations with large language models to reduce waste and enable modular adaptability. His approach reduced drywall waste and turnover costs while preserving modular elements for future adaptability.

The session underscored AI's ability to enhance efficiency, creativity, and sustainability in architecture. Discussions ranged from predictive design and data-driven estimation to visualization and modular adaptability, showcasing diverse applications of AI in the field. Each speaker highlighted innovative methods to address complex design challenges, demonstrating AI's growing role in shaping the future of architecture.

--- SUMMARY ---

Summary

The "Computational Design" session at AI in AEC 2026 explored the transformative role of artificial intelligence in reshaping workflows within architecture, engineering, and construction (AEC). Featuring speakers such as Miro Lehtimäki, Dirk Münzner, Alejandro Pacheco Diéguez, Joonas Helminen, Kristoffer Berglund, Christop Berkmiller, Shicong Cao, Tuomo Palomaa, Manoj Bala Radhakrishna, and Ramiz Mohareb, the session highlighted innovative applications of AI across diverse domains. Discussions ranged from AI-enhanced post-occupancy evaluations to parametric modeling in Grasshopper, with Kwaki, an AI plugin, streamlining computational design processes. Real-world projects like the Lake Constance tunnel demonstrated AI-driven infrastructure automation, while Lux, a daylight simulation platform, integrated real-time daylight factor analysis into IFC models, enabling architects to optimize designs efficiently. Predictive analytics for daylight autonomy were incorporated into Revit workflows, offering rapid simulations for enhanced decision-making. Hybrid approaches combining OCR and computer vision were showcased in wood truss analysis, and AI-powered semantic comparison engines improved QA/QC processes in rebar design verification. Feedback loops in design optimization facilitated iterative improvements, while data-driven urban health insights revealed how environmental factors like parks and cafes impact well-being more than hospital density. Challenges in BIM standardization underscored the need for consistent methodologies, and sustainable AI practices addressed energy and resource consumption in computational workflows. The session also emphasized AI's growing role in urban planning and environmental design, demonstrating its potential to enhance collaboration, reduce manual effort, and drive innovation across AEC disciplines. Through these discussions, the speakers illustrated how AI is not only a tool for efficiency but also a catalyst for reimagining the future of design and construction.

What automation delivers? So if we treated these as individual point solutions. The start forms, the safety observations and maybe some of the analytics on an existing database. The you will probably get the same result without a platform approach, right? You could treat them independently and you would probably see something like this. You get 10x reporting volume. You get a 2 x near miss capture. The cycle time goes from 2 days from that team manually

translating paper forms into Excel goes from 2 days to 3 minutes, and that's probably the most important one because we're shortening the time from something that happened in the field and was reported to to the time when somebody actually does something to resolve that problem.

And of course, we get about 95% reduction in manual work through automation. These numbers are super impressive and leadership loves us for them, but this is nowhere near where the value is. The value actually comes from what the platform unlocks. So you don't just get automation. You have fragmented data on the left. You have the platform in the middle that takes the data, and ingests it, it extracts it, enriches it, classifies it, stores it at indexes it so AI can

read it and then it can generate new content on it, and it's all traceable and you can evaluate every single step of the, of, of the process. The result is not just automation. The result is this compounding effect where you're building institutional memory. You're enabling cross-system intelligence, predictive analytics, trend analysis, and deep auditability when something goes wrong.

You don't get this from Point Solutions because Pot Solutions permanently disconnect the left side from the from the right. You need a platform approach to be able to connect those two things and create compounding value over time.

So I've been talking about safety for this entire presentation, but the platform doesn't care about the domain. The platform cares about knowledge. It cares about data. It cares about orchestration. So we can extend exactly the same infrastructure, the same underlying platform stack to multiple domains, especially document heavy domains, manual workflows, and so on.
